<div class="mw-body-content mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Capacity for consciously making sense of things</div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}</style><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">This article is about the human faculty of reason and rationality. For other uses, see <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Reason_(disambiguation)" title="Reason (disambiguation)">Reason (disambiguation)</a>.</div>

<img alt="" class="noviewer" data-file-height="500" data-file-width="326" decoding="async" height="16" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Socrates.png/10px-Socrates.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Socrates.png/15px-Socrates.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Socrates.png/21px-Socrates.png 2x" width="10"/> <a href="/wiki/Portal:Philosophy" title="Portal:Philosophy">Philosophy portal</a></td></tr><tr><td class="sidebar-navbar"><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1063604349">.mw-parser-output .navbar{display:inline;font-size:88%;font-weight:normal}.mw-parser-output .navbar-collapse{float:left;text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .navbar-boxtext{word-spacing:0}.mw-parser-output .navbar ul{display:inline-block;white-space:nowrap;line-height:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-brackets::before{margin-right:-0.125em;content:"[ "}.mw-parser-output .navbar-brackets::after{margin-left:-0.125em;content:" ]"}.mw-parser-output .navbar li{word-spacing:-0.125em}.mw-parser-output .navbar a>span,.mw-parser-output .navbar a>abbr{text-decoration:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-mini abbr{font-variant:small-caps;border-bottom:none;text-decoration:none;cursor:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-ct-full{font-size:114%;margin:0 7em}.mw-parser-output .navbar-ct-mini{font-size:114%;margin:0 4em}</style><div class="navbar plainlinks hlist navbar-mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Philosophy_sidebar" title="Template:Philosophy sidebar"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Philosophy_sidebar" title="Template talk:Philosophy sidebar"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Philosophy_sidebar&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p><b>Reason</b> is the capacity of <a href="/wiki/Consciousness" title="Consciousness">consciously</a> applying <a href="/wiki/Logic" title="Logic">logic</a> by <a href="/wiki/Logical_consequence" title="Logical consequence">drawing conclusions</a> from new or existing <a href="/wiki/Information" title="Information">information</a>, with the aim of seeking the <a href="/wiki/Truth" title="Truth">truth</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup> It is closely associated with such characteristically <a href="/wiki/Human" title="Human">human</a> activities as <a href="/wiki/Philosophy" title="Philosophy">philosophy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Science" title="Science">science</a>, <a href="/wiki/Language" title="Language">language</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mathematics" title="Mathematics">mathematics</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Art" title="Art">art</a>, and is normally considered to be a distinguishing ability possessed by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Humans" title="Humans">humans</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup> Reason is sometimes referred to as <a href="/wiki/Rationality" title="Rationality">rationality</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup>
</p><p><b>Reasoning</b> is associated with the acts of <a href="/wiki/Thought" title="Thought">thinking</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cognition" title="Cognition">cognition</a>, and involves the use of one's <a href="/wiki/Intellect" title="Intellect">intellect</a>. The field of <a href="/wiki/Logic" title="Logic">logic</a> studies the ways in which humans can use <b>formal reasoning</b> to produce <a href="/wiki/Validity_(logic)" title="Validity (logic)">logically valid</a> <a href="/wiki/Argument" title="Argument">arguments</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup> Reasoning may be subdivided into <a href="/wiki/Logical_form" title="Logical form">forms</a> of <a href="/wiki/Logical_reasoning" title="Logical reasoning">logical reasoning</a>, such as: <a href="/wiki/Deductive_reasoning" title="Deductive reasoning">deductive reasoning</a>, <a href="/wiki/Inductive_reasoning" title="Inductive reasoning">inductive reasoning</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Abductive_reasoning" title="Abductive reasoning">abductive reasoning</a>. <a href="/wiki/Aristotle" title="Aristotle">Aristotle</a> drew a distinction between logical <b>discursive reasoning</b> (reason proper), and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Intuition_(psychology)" title="Intuition (psychology)">intuitive reasoning</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup> in which the reasoning process through intuition—however valid—may tend toward the personal and the subjectively opaque. In some social and political settings logical and intuitive modes of reasoning may clash, while in other contexts intuition and formal reason are seen as complementary rather than adversarial. For example, in <a href="/wiki/Mathematics" title="Mathematics">mathematics</a>, intuition is often necessary for the creative processes involved with arriving at a <a href="/wiki/Formal_proof" title="Formal proof">formal proof</a>, arguably the most difficult of formal reasoning tasks.
</p><p>Reasoning, like habit or <a href="/wiki/Intuition" title="Intuition">intuition</a>, is one of the ways by which thinking moves from one idea to a related idea. For example, reasoning is the means by which rational individuals understand sensory information from their environments, or conceptualize abstract dichotomies such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cause" title="Cause">cause</a> and <a href="/wiki/Causality" title="Causality">effect</a>, <a href="/wiki/Truth" title="Truth">truth</a> and <a href="/wiki/False_(logic)" title="False (logic)">falsehood</a>, or ideas regarding notions of <a href="/wiki/Good_and_evil" title="Good and evil">good or evil</a>. Reasoning, as a part of <a href="/wiki/Executive_functions" title="Executive functions">executive decision making</a>, is also closely identified with the ability to self-consciously change, in terms of <a href="/wiki/Goal" title="Goal">goals</a>, <a href="/wiki/Belief" title="Belief">beliefs</a>, <a href="/wiki/Attitude_(psychology)" title="Attitude (psychology)">attitudes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tradition" title="Tradition">traditions</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Institution" title="Institution">institutions</a>, and therefore with the capacity for <a href="/wiki/Freedom" title="Freedom">freedom</a> and <a href="/wiki/Self-determination" title="Self-determination">self-determination</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup>
</p><p>In contrast to the use of "reason" as an <a href="/wiki/Noun#Concrete_nouns_and_abstract_nouns" title="Noun">abstract noun</a>, <a href="/wiki/Reason_(argument)" title="Reason (argument)">a reason</a> is a consideration given which either explains or justifies events, phenomena, or <a href="/wiki/Behavior" title="Behavior">behavior</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mw_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-mw-8">[8]</a></sup> Reasons justify decisions, reasons support explanations of natural phenomena; reasons can be given to explain the actions (conduct) of individuals.
</p><p>Using reason, or reasoning, can also be described more plainly as providing good, or the best, reasons. For example, when evaluating a moral decision, "morality is, at the very least, the effort to guide one's conduct by <i>reason</i>—that is, doing what there are the best reasons for doing—while giving equal [and impartial] weight to the interests of all those affected by what one does."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Psychologist" title="Psychologist">Psychologists</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cognitive_science" title="Cognitive science">cognitive scientists</a> have attempted to study and explain <a href="/wiki/Psychology_of_reasoning" title="Psychology of reasoning">how people reason</a>, e.g. which cognitive and neural processes are engaged, and how cultural factors affect the inferences that people draw. The field of <a href="/wiki/Automated_reasoning" title="Automated reasoning">automated reasoning</a> studies how reasoning may or may not be modeled computationally. <a href="/wiki/Comparative_psychology" title="Comparative psychology">Animal psychology</a> considers the question of whether animals other than humans can reason.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology_and_related_words">Etymology and related words</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Reason&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Etymology and related words">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>In the <a href="/wiki/English_language" title="English language">English language</a> and other modern <a href="/wiki/Languages_of_Europe" title="Languages of Europe">European languages</a>, "reason", and related words, represent words which have always been used to translate Latin and classical Greek terms in the sense of their philosophical usage.
</p>
<ul><li>The original <a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">Greek</a> term was <span title="Ancient Greek (to 1453)-language text"><span lang="grc">"λόγος"</span></span> <i><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/logos#Greek" title="wikt:logos">logos</a></i>, the root of the modern English word "<a href="/wiki/Logic" title="Logic">logic</a>" but also a word which could mean for example "speech" or "explanation" or an "account" (of money handled).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup></li>
<li>As a philosophical term <i>logos</i> was translated in its non-linguistic senses in <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a> as <i><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/ratio#Latin" title="wikt:ratio">ratio</a></i>. This was originally not just a translation used for philosophy, but was also commonly a translation for <i>logos</i> in the sense of an account of money.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/French_language" title="French language">French</a> <i><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/raison" title="wikt:raison">raison</a></i> is derived directly from Latin, and this is the direct source of the English word "reason".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mw_8-1"><a href="#cite_note-mw-8">[8]</a></sup></li></ul>
<p>The earliest major philosophers to publish in English, such as <a href="/wiki/Francis_Bacon" title="Francis Bacon">Francis Bacon</a>, <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Hobbes" title="Thomas Hobbes">Thomas Hobbes</a>, and <a href="/wiki/John_Locke" title="John Locke">John Locke</a> also routinely wrote in Latin and French, and compared their terms to Greek, treating the words "<i>logos</i>", "<i>ratio</i>", "<i>raison</i>" and "reason" as interchangeable. The meaning of the word "reason" in senses such as "human reason" also overlaps to a large extent with "<a href="/wiki/Rationality" title="Rationality">rationality</a>" and the adjective of "reason" in philosophical contexts is normally "<a href="/wiki/Rationality" title="Rationality">rational</a>", rather than "reasoned" or "reasonable".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup> Some philosophers, <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Hobbes" title="Thomas Hobbes">Thomas Hobbes</a> for example, also used the word <b>ratiocination</b> as a synonym for "reasoning".
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Philosophical_history">Philosophical history<span class="anchor" id="History_of_reasoning"></span></span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Reason&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Philosophical history">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Goya_Caprichos3.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="979" data-file-width="650" decoding="async" height="331" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/Goya_Caprichos3.jpg/220px-Goya_Caprichos3.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/Goya_Caprichos3.jpg/330px-Goya_Caprichos3.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/Goya_Caprichos3.jpg/440px-Goya_Caprichos3.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Goya_Caprichos3.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Francisco_de_Goya" title="Francisco de Goya">Francisco de Goya</a>, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Sleep_of_Reason_Produces_Monsters" title="The Sleep of Reason Produces Monsters">The Sleep of Reason Produces Monsters</a></i> (<i>El sueño de la razón produce monstruos</i>), c. 1797</div></div></div>
<p>The proposal that reason gives humanity a special position in nature has been argued to be a defining characteristic of western <a href="/wiki/Philosophy" title="Philosophy">philosophy</a> and later western <a href="/wiki/History_of_science#Modern_science" title="History of science">modern science</a>, starting with classical Greece. Philosophy can be described as a way of life based upon reason, and in the other direction, reason has been one of the major subjects of philosophical discussion since ancient times. Reason is often said to be <a href="/wiki/Reflexivity_(social_theory)" title="Reflexivity (social theory)">reflexive</a>, or "self-correcting", and the critique of reason has been a persistent theme in philosophy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jürgen_Habermas_1990_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-Jürgen_Habermas_1990-13">[13]</a></sup> It has been defined in different ways, at different times, by different thinkers about human nature.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Classical_philosophy">Classical philosophy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Reason&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Classical philosophy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>For many classical <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Philosophers" title="Philosophers">philosophers</a>, nature was understood <a href="/wiki/Teleology" title="Teleology">teleologically</a>, meaning that every type of thing had a definitive purpose that fit within a natural order that was itself understood to have aims. Perhaps starting with <a href="/wiki/Pythagoras" title="Pythagoras">Pythagoras</a> or <a href="/wiki/Heraclitus" title="Heraclitus">Heraclitus</a>, the cosmos is even said to have reason.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup> Reason, by this account, is not just one characteristic that humans happen to have, and that influences happiness amongst other characteristics. Reason was considered of higher stature than other characteristics of human nature, such as sociability, because it is something humans share with nature itself, linking an apparently immortal part of the human mind with the divine order of the cosmos itself. Within the human <a href="/wiki/Mind" title="Mind">mind</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Soul_(spirit)" title="Soul (spirit)">soul</a> (<i><a href="/wiki/Psyche_(psychology)" title="Psyche (psychology)">psyche</a></i>), reason was described by <a href="/wiki/Plato" title="Plato">Plato</a> as being the natural monarch which should rule over the other parts, such as spiritedness (<i><a href="/wiki/Thumos" title="Thumos">thumos</a></i>) and the passions. <a href="/wiki/Aristotle" title="Aristotle">Aristotle</a>, Plato's student, defined human beings as <a href="/wiki/Rational_animal" title="Rational animal">rational animals</a>, emphasizing reason as a characteristic of <a href="/wiki/Human_nature" title="Human nature">human nature</a>. He <i>defined</i> the highest human happiness or well being (<i><a href="/wiki/Eudaimonia" title="Eudaimonia">eudaimonia</a></i>) as a life which is lived consistently, excellently, and completely in accordance with reason.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup>
</p><p>The conclusions to be drawn from the discussions of Aristotle and Plato on this matter are amongst the most debated in the history of philosophy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Davidson_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-Davidson-16">[16]</a></sup> But teleological accounts such as Aristotle's were highly influential for those who attempt to explain reason in a way that is consistent with <a href="/wiki/Monotheism" title="Monotheism">monotheism</a> and the immortality and divinity of the human soul. For example, in the <a href="/wiki/Neoplatonism" title="Neoplatonism">neoplatonist</a> account of <a href="/wiki/Plotinus" title="Plotinus">Plotinus</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Cosmos" title="Cosmos">cosmos</a> has one soul, which is the seat of all reason, and the souls of all individual humans are part of this soul. Reason is for Plotinus both the provider of form to material things, and the light which brings individuals souls back into line with their source.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Christian_and_Islamic_philosophy">Christian and Islamic philosophy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Reason&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Christian and Islamic philosophy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The classical view of reason, like many important Neoplatonic and Stoic ideas, was readily adopted by the early Church <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup> as the Church Fathers saw Greek Philosophy as an indispensable instrument given to mankind so that we may understand revelation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup> For example, the greatest among the early saint <a href="/wiki/Church_Fathers" title="Church Fathers">Church Fathers</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Doctors_of_the_Church" title="Doctors of the Church">Doctors of the Church</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Augustine_of_Hippo" title="Augustine of Hippo">Augustine of Hippo</a>, <a href="/wiki/Basil_of_Caesarea" title="Basil of Caesarea">Basil of Caesarea</a> and <a href="/wiki/Gregory_of_Nyssa" title="Gregory of Nyssa">Gregory of Nyssa</a> were as much Neoplatonic philosophers as they were Christian theologians and adopted the Neoplatonic view of human reason together with the associated implications for our relationship to creation, to ourselves and to God. Such Neoplatonist accounts of the rational part of the human soul were also standard amongst medieval Islamic philosophers and remain important in <a href="/wiki/Iranian_philosophy" title="Iranian philosophy">Iranian philosophy</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Davidson_16-1"><a href="#cite_note-Davidson-16">[16]</a></sup> As European intellectualism recovered from the post-Roman Dark Ages, the Christian <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Patristic" title="Patristic">Patristic</a> heritage and the influence of the great Islamic scholars such as <a href="/wiki/Averroes" title="Averroes">Averroes</a> and <a href="/wiki/Avicenna" title="Avicenna">Avicenna</a> produced the Scholastic (see <a href="/wiki/Scholasticism" title="Scholasticism">Scholasticism</a>) view of reason from which our modern idea of this concept has developed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup> Among the Scholastics who relied on the classical concept of reason for the development of their doctrines, none were more influential than <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Saint_Thomas_Aquinas" title="Saint Thomas Aquinas">Saint Thomas Aquinas</a>, who put this concept at the heart of his <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Natural_Law" title="Natural Law">Natural Law</a>. In this doctrine, Thomas concludes that because humans have reason and because reason is a spark of the divine, every single human life is invaluable, all humans are equal and every human is born with an intrinsic and permanent set of basic rights.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup> On this foundation, the idea of human rights would later be constructed by Spanish theologians at the <a href="/wiki/School_of_Salamanca" title="School of Salamanca">School of Salamanca</a>. Other Scholastics, such as <a href="/wiki/Roger_Bacon" title="Roger Bacon">Roger Bacon</a> and <a href="/wiki/Albertus_Magnus" title="Albertus Magnus">Albertus Magnus</a>, following the example of Islamic scholars such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alhazen" title="Alhazen">Alhazen</a>, emphasised reason an intrinsic human ability to decode the created order and the structures that underlie our experienced physical reality. This interpretation of reason was instrumental to the development of the scientific method in the early Universities of the high Middle Ages.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Subject-centred_reason_in_early_modern_philosophy">Subject-centred reason in early modern philosophy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Reason&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Subject-centred reason in early modern philosophy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Early_modern_era" title="Early modern era">early modern era</a> was marked by a number of significant changes in the understanding of reason, starting in <a href="/wiki/Europe" title="Europe">Europe</a>. One of the most important of these changes involved a change in the <a href="/wiki/Metaphysics" title="Metaphysics">metaphysical</a> understanding of human beings. Scientists and philosophers began to question the teleological understanding of the world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup> Nature was no longer assumed to be human-like, with its own aims or reason, and human nature was no longer assumed to work according to anything other than the same "<a href="/wiki/Natural_law" title="Natural law">laws of nature</a>" which affect inanimate things. This new understanding eventually displaced the previous <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/World_view" title="World view">world view</a> that derived from a spiritual understanding of the universe.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Frans_Hals_-_Portret_van_Ren%C3%A9_Descartes.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1000" data-file-width="817" decoding="async" height="245" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Frans_Hals_-_Portret_van_Ren%C3%A9_Descartes.jpg/200px-Frans_Hals_-_Portret_van_Ren%C3%A9_Descartes.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Frans_Hals_-_Portret_van_Ren%C3%A9_Descartes.jpg/300px-Frans_Hals_-_Portret_van_Ren%C3%A9_Descartes.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Frans_Hals_-_Portret_van_Ren%C3%A9_Descartes.jpg/400px-Frans_Hals_-_Portret_van_Ren%C3%A9_Descartes.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Frans_Hals_-_Portret_van_Ren%C3%A9_Descartes.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>René Descartes</div></div></div>
<p>Accordingly, in the 17th century, <a href="/wiki/Ren%C3%A9_Descartes" title="René Descartes">René Descartes</a> explicitly rejected the traditional notion of humans as "rational animals", suggesting instead that they are nothing more than "thinking things" along the lines of other "things" in nature. Any grounds of knowledge outside that understanding was, therefore, subject to doubt.
</p><p>In his search for a foundation of all possible knowledge, Descartes deliberately decided to throw into doubt <i>all</i> knowledge – <i>except</i> that of the mind itself in the process of thinking:
</p>
<blockquote><p>At this time I admit nothing that is not necessarily true. I am therefore precisely nothing but a thinking thing; that is a mind, or intellect, or understanding, or reason – words of whose meanings I was previously ignorant.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<p>This eventually became known as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Epistemological" title="Epistemological">epistemological</a> or "subject-centred" reason, because it is based on the <i>knowing subject</i>, who perceives the rest of the world and itself as a set of objects to be studied, and successfully mastered by applying the knowledge accumulated through such study. Breaking with tradition and many thinkers after him, Descartes explicitly did not divide the incorporeal soul into parts, such as reason and intellect, describing them as one indivisible incorporeal entity.
</p><p>A contemporary of Descartes, <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Hobbes" title="Thomas Hobbes">Thomas Hobbes</a> described reason as a broader version of "addition and subtraction" which is not limited to numbers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup> This understanding of reason is sometimes termed "calculative" reason. Similar to Descartes, Hobbes asserted that "No discourse whatsoever, can end in absolute knowledge of fact, past, or to come" but that "sense and memory" is absolute knowledge.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the late 17th century, through the 18th century, <a href="/wiki/John_Locke" title="John Locke">John Locke</a> and <a href="/wiki/David_Hume" title="David Hume">David Hume</a> developed Descartes's line of thought still further. Hume took it in an especially <a href="/wiki/Skepticism" title="Skepticism">skeptical</a> direction, proposing that there could be no possibility of <a href="/wiki/Deductive_reasoning" title="Deductive reasoning">deducing</a> relationships of cause and effect, and therefore no knowledge is based on reasoning alone, even if it seems otherwise.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup>
</p><p>Hume famously remarked that, "We speak not strictly and philosophically when we talk of the combat of passion and of reason. Reason is, and ought only to be the slave of the passions, and can never pretend to any other office than to serve and obey them."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup> Hume also took his definition of reason to unorthodox extremes by arguing, unlike his predecessors, that human reason is not qualitatively different from either simply conceiving individual ideas, or from judgments associating two ideas,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup> and that "reason is nothing but a wonderful and unintelligible instinct in our souls, which carries us along a certain train of ideas, and endows them with particular qualities, according to their particular situations and relations."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup> It followed from this that animals have reason, only much less complex than human reason.
</p><p>In the 18th century, <a href="/wiki/Immanuel_Kant" title="Immanuel Kant">Immanuel Kant</a> attempted to show that Hume was wrong by demonstrating that a "<a href="/wiki/Transcendental_arguments" title="Transcendental arguments">transcendental</a>" self, or "I", was a necessary condition of all experience. Therefore, suggested Kant, on the basis of such a self, it is in fact possible to reason both about the conditions and limits of human knowledge. And so long as these limits are respected, reason can be the vehicle of morality, justice, aesthetics, theories of knowledge (<a href="/wiki/Epistemology" title="Epistemology">epistemology</a>), and understanding.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Substantive_and_formal_reason">Substantive and formal reason</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Reason&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Substantive and formal reason">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In the formulation of Kant, who wrote some of the most influential modern treatises on the subject, the great achievement of reason (<a href="/wiki/German_language" title="German language">German</a>: <i lang="de">Vernunft</i>) is that it is able to exercise a kind of universal law-making. Kant was able therefore to reformulate the basis of moral-practical, theoretical and aesthetic reasoning, on "universal" laws.
</p><p>Here <a href="/wiki/Practical_reason" title="Practical reason">practical reasoning</a> is the self-legislating or self-governing formulation of universal <a href="/wiki/Norm_(philosophy)" title="Norm (philosophy)">norms</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Theory" title="Theory">theoretical</a> reasoning the way humans posit universal <a href="/wiki/Natural_law" title="Natural law">laws of nature</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup>
</p><p>Under practical reason, the moral <a href="/wiki/Autonomy" title="Autonomy">autonomy</a> or freedom of human beings depends on their ability to behave according to laws that are given to them by the proper exercise of that reason. This contrasted with earlier forms of morality, which depended on <a href="/wiki/Religion" title="Religion">religious understanding</a> and interpretation, or <a href="/wiki/Nature" title="Nature">nature</a> for their substance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup>
</p><p>According to Kant, in a free society each individual must be able to pursue their goals however they see fit, so long as their actions conform to principles given by reason. He formulated such a principle, called the "<a href="/wiki/Categorical_imperative" title="Categorical imperative">categorical imperative</a>", which would justify an action only if it could be universalized:
</p>
<blockquote><p>Act only according to that maxim whereby you can, at the same time, will that it should become a universal law.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ellington_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ellington-34">[34]</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<p>In contrast to Hume then, Kant insists that reason itself (German <i><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/Vernunft" title="wikt:Vernunft">Vernunft</a></i>) has natural ends itself, the solution to the metaphysical problems, especially the discovery of the foundations of morality. Kant claimed that this problem could be solved with his "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Transcendental_logic" title="Transcendental logic">transcendental logic</a>" which unlike normal logic is not just an instrument, which can be used indifferently, as it was for Aristotle, but a theoretical science in its own right and the basis of all the others.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup>
</p><p>According to <a href="/wiki/J%C3%BCrgen_Habermas" title="Jürgen Habermas">Jürgen Habermas</a>, the "substantive unity" of reason has dissolved in modern times, such that it can no longer answer the question "How should I live?" Instead, the unity of reason has to be strictly formal, or "procedural". He thus described reason as a group of three autonomous spheres (on the model of Kant's three critiques):
</p>
<ol><li><i>Cognitive–instrumental reason</i> is the kind of reason employed by the sciences. It is used to observe events, to predict and control outcomes, and to intervene in the world on the basis of its hypotheses;</li>
<li><i>Moral–practical reason</i> is what we use to deliberate and discuss issues in the moral and political realm, according to universalizable procedures (similar to Kant's categorical imperative); and</li>
<li><i>Aesthetic reason</i> is typically found in works of art and literature, and encompasses the novel ways of seeing the world and interpreting things that those practices embody.</li></ol>
<p>For Habermas, these three spheres are the domain of experts, and therefore need to be mediated with the "<a href="/wiki/Lifeworld" title="Lifeworld">lifeworld</a>" by philosophers. In drawing such a picture of reason, Habermas hoped to demonstrate that the substantive unity of reason, which in pre-modern societies had been able to answer questions about the good life, could be made up for by the unity of reason's formalizable procedures.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="The_critique_of_reason">The critique of reason</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Reason&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: The critique of reason">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Johann_Georg_Hamann" title="Johann Georg Hamann">Hamann</a>, <a href="/wiki/Johann_Gottfried_Herder" title="Johann Gottfried Herder">Herder</a>, <a href="/wiki/Immanuel_Kant" title="Immanuel Kant">Kant</a>, <a href="/wiki/Georg_Wilhelm_Friedrich_Hegel" title="Georg Wilhelm Friedrich Hegel">Hegel</a>, <a href="/wiki/S%C3%B8ren_Kierkegaard" title="Søren Kierkegaard">Kierkegaard</a>, <a href="/wiki/Friedrich_Nietzsche" title="Friedrich Nietzsche">Nietzsche</a>, <a href="/wiki/Martin_Heidegger" title="Martin Heidegger">Heidegger</a>, <a href="/wiki/Michel_Foucault" title="Michel Foucault">Foucault</a>, <a href="/wiki/Richard_Rorty" title="Richard Rorty">Rorty</a>, and many other philosophers have contributed to a debate about what reason means, or ought to mean. Some, like Kierkegaard, Nietzsche, and Rorty, are skeptical about subject-centred, universal, or instrumental reason, and even skeptical toward reason as a whole. Others, including Hegel, believe that it has obscured the importance of <a href="/wiki/Intersubjectivity" title="Intersubjectivity">intersubjectivity</a>, or "spirit" in human life, and attempt to reconstruct a model of what reason should be.
</p><p>Some thinkers, e.g. Foucault, believe there are other <i>forms</i> of reason, neglected but essential to modern life, and to our understanding of what it means to live a life according to reason.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jürgen_Habermas_1990_13-1"><a href="#cite_note-Jürgen_Habermas_1990-13">[13]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the last several decades, a number of proposals have been made to "re-orient" this critique of reason, or to recognize the "other voices" or "new departments" of reason:
</p><p>For example, in opposition to subject-centred reason, Habermas has proposed a model of <a href="/wiki/Communicative_rationality" title="Communicative rationality">communicative reason</a> that sees it as an essentially cooperative activity, based on the fact of linguistic <a href="/wiki/Intersubjectivity" title="Intersubjectivity">intersubjectivity</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Nikolas_Kompridis" title="Nikolas Kompridis">Nikolas Kompridis</a> has proposed a widely encompassing view of reason as "that ensemble of practices that contributes to the opening and preserving of openness" in human affairs, and a focus on reason's possibilities for social change.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup>
</p><p>The philosopher <a href="/wiki/Charles_Taylor_(philosopher)" title="Charles Taylor (philosopher)">Charles Taylor</a>, influenced by the 20th century German philosopher <a href="/wiki/Martin_Heidegger" title="Martin Heidegger">Martin Heidegger</a>, has proposed that reason ought to include the faculty of <a href="/wiki/World_disclosure" title="World disclosure">disclosure</a>, which is tied to the way we make sense of things in everyday life, as a new "department" of reason.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the essay "What is Enlightenment?", Michel Foucault proposed a concept of critique based on Kant's distinction between "private" and "public" uses of reason. This distinction, as suggested, has two dimensions:
</p>
<ul><li><i>Private reason</i> is the reason that is used when an individual is "a cog in a machine" or when one "has a role to play in society and jobs to do: to be a soldier, to have taxes to pay, to be in charge of a parish, to be a civil servant".</li>
<li><i>Public reason</i> is the reason used "when one is reasoning as a reasonable being (and not as a cog in a machine), when one is reasoning as a member of reasonable humanity". In these circumstances, "the use of reason must be free and public."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup></li></ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Reason_compared_to_related_concepts">Reason compared to related concepts</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Reason&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Reason compared to related concepts">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Compared_to_logic">Compared to logic</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Reason&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Compared to logic">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Logic" title="Logic">Logic</a></div>
<p>The terms <i>logic</i> or <i>logical</i> are sometimes used as if they were identical with the term <i>reason</i> or with the concept of being <i>rational</i>, or sometimes logic is seen as the most pure or the defining form of reason: "Logic is about reasoning—about going from premises to a conclusion. ... When you do logic, you try to clarify reasoning and separate good from bad reasoning."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup> In modern <a href="/wiki/Economics" title="Economics">economics</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rational_choice_theory" title="Rational choice theory">rational choice</a> is assumed to equate to logically <a href="/wiki/Consistency" title="Consistency">consistent</a> choice.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup>
</p><p>Reason and logic can however be thought of as distinct, although logic is one important aspect of reason. Author <a href="/wiki/Douglas_Hofstadter" title="Douglas Hofstadter">Douglas Hofstadter</a>, in <i><a href="/wiki/G%C3%B6del,_Escher,_Bach" title="Gödel, Escher, Bach">Gödel, Escher, Bach</a></i>, characterizes the distinction in this way: Logic is done inside a system while reason is done outside the system by such methods as skipping steps, working backward, drawing diagrams, looking at examples, or seeing what happens if you change the rules of the system.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup> Psychologists Mark H. Bickard and Robert L. Campbell argued that "rationality cannot be simply assimilated to logicality"; they noted that "human knowledge of logic and logical systems has developed" over time through reasoning, and logical systems "can't construct new logical systems more powerful than themselves", so reasoning and rationality must involve more than a system of logic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Moshman2004_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-Moshman2004-45">[45]</a></sup> Psychologist David Moshman, citing Bickhard and Campbell, argued for a "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Metacognitive" title="Metacognitive">metacognitive</a> conception of rationality" in which a person's development of reason "involves increasing consciousness and control of logical and other inferences".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Moshman2004_45-1"><a href="#cite_note-Moshman2004-45">[45]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup>
</p><p>Reason is a type of <a href="/wiki/Thought" title="Thought">thought</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Logic" title="Logic">logic</a> involves the attempt to describe a system of formal rules or norms of appropriate reasoning.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Moshman2004_45-2"><a href="#cite_note-Moshman2004-45">[45]</a></sup> The oldest surviving writing to explicitly consider the rules by which reason operates are the works of the <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greece" title="Ancient Greece">Greek</a> <a href="/wiki/Philosopher" title="Philosopher">philosopher</a> <a href="/wiki/Aristotle" title="Aristotle">Aristotle</a>, especially <i>Prior Analysis</i> and <i>Posterior Analysis</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template noprint Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:No_original_research#Primary,_secondary_and_tertiary_sources" title="Wikipedia:No original research"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable secondary sources. (August 2021)">non-primary source needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Although the Ancient Greeks had no separate word for logic as distinct from language and reason, Aristotle's <a href="/wiki/Neologism" title="Neologism">newly coined word</a> "<a href="/wiki/Syllogism" title="Syllogism">syllogism</a>" (<i>syllogismos</i>) identified logic clearly for the first time as a distinct field of study.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup> When Aristotle referred to "the logical" (<i>hē logikē</i>), he was referring more broadly to rational thought.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Reason_compared_to_cause-and-effect_thinking.2C_and_symbolic_thinking"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Reason_compared_to_cause-and-effect_thinking,_and_symbolic_thinking">Reason compared to cause-and-effect thinking, and symbolic thinking</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Reason&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Reason compared to cause-and-effect thinking, and symbolic thinking">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Causality" title="Causality">Causality</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Symbols" title="Symbols">Symbols</a></div>
<p>As pointed out by philosophers such as Hobbes, Locke and Hume, some animals are also clearly capable of a type of "<a href="/wiki/Association_(psychology)" title="Association (psychology)">associative thinking</a>", even to the extent of associating causes and effects. A dog once kicked, can learn how to recognize the warning signs and avoid being kicked in the future, but this does not mean the dog has reason in any strict sense of the word. It also does not mean that humans acting on the basis of experience or habit are using their reason.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup>
</p><p>Human reason requires more than being able to associate two ideas, even if those two ideas might be described by a reasoning human as a cause and an effect, perceptions of smoke, for example, and memories of fire. For reason to be involved, the association of smoke and the fire would have to be thought through in a way which can be explained, for example as cause and effect. In the explanation of <a href="/wiki/John_Locke" title="John Locke">Locke</a>, for example, reason requires the mental use of a third idea in order to make this comparison by use of <a href="/wiki/Syllogism" title="Syllogism">syllogism</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup>
</p><p>More generally, reason in the strict sense requires the ability to create and manipulate a system of <a href="/wiki/Symbol" title="Symbol">symbols</a>, as well as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Semiotic_elements_and_classes_of_signs_(Peirce)#II._Icon,_index,_symbol" title="Semiotic elements and classes of signs (Peirce)">indices and icons</a>, according to <a href="/wiki/Charles_Sanders_Peirce" title="Charles Sanders Peirce">Charles Sanders Peirce</a>, the symbols having only a nominal, though habitual, connection to either smoke or fire.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup> One example of such a system of artificial symbols and signs is <a href="/wiki/Language" title="Language">language</a>.
</p><p>The connection of reason to symbolic thinking has been expressed in different ways by philosophers. <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Hobbes" title="Thomas Hobbes">Thomas Hobbes</a> described the creation of "Markes, or Notes of remembrance" (<i><a href="/wiki/Leviathan_(Hobbes_book)" title="Leviathan (Hobbes book)">Leviathan</a></i> Ch. 4) as <i>speech</i>. He used the word <i>speech</i> as an English version of the Greek word <i><a href="/wiki/Logos" title="Logos">logos</a></i> so that speech did not need to be communicated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup> When communicated, such speech becomes language, and the marks or notes or remembrance are called "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sign_(linguistics)" title="Sign (linguistics)">Signes</a>" by Hobbes. Going further back, although Aristotle is a source of the idea that only humans have reason (<i>logos</i>), he does mention that animals with imagination, for whom sense perceptions can persist, come closest to having something like reasoning and <i><a href="/wiki/Nous" title="Nous">nous</a></i>, and even uses the word "logos" in one place to describe the distinctions which animals can perceive in such cases.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Reason.2C_imagination.2C_mimesis.2C_and_memory"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Reason,_imagination,_mimesis,_and_memory">Reason, imagination, mimesis, and memory</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Reason&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Reason, imagination, mimesis, and memory">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Imagination" title="Imagination">Imagination</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mimesis" title="Mimesis">Mimesis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Memory" title="Memory">Memory</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Recollection" title="Recollection">Recollection</a></div>
<p>Reason and <a href="/wiki/Imagination" title="Imagination">imagination</a> rely on similar <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mental_processes" title="Mental processes">mental processes</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup> Imagination is not only found in humans. Aristotle, for example, stated that <i>phantasia</i> (imagination: that which can hold images or <i>phantasmata</i>) and <i>phronein</i> (a type of thinking that can judge and understand in some sense) also exist in some animals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup> According to him, both are related to the primary perceptive ability of animals, which gathers the perceptions of different senses and defines the order of the things that are perceived without distinguishing universals, and without deliberation or <i>logos</i>. But this is not yet reason, because human imagination is different.
</p><p>The recent modern writings of <a href="/wiki/Terrence_Deacon" title="Terrence Deacon">Terrence Deacon</a> and <a href="/wiki/Merlin_Donald" title="Merlin Donald">Merlin Donald</a>, writing about the <a href="/wiki/Origin_of_language" title="Origin of language">origin of language</a>, also connect reason connected to not only <a href="/wiki/Language" title="Language">language</a>, but also <a href="/wiki/Mimesis" title="Mimesis">mimesis</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup> More specifically they describe the ability to create <a href="/wiki/Language" title="Language">language</a> as part of an internal modeling of <a href="/wiki/Reality" title="Reality">reality</a> specific to humankind. Other results are <a href="/wiki/Consciousness" title="Consciousness">consciousness</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Imagination" title="Imagination">imagination</a> or <a href="/wiki/Fantasy" title="Fantasy">fantasy</a>. In contrast, modern proponents of a genetic predisposition to language itself include <a href="/wiki/Noam_Chomsky" title="Noam Chomsky">Noam Chomsky</a> and <a href="/wiki/Steven_Pinker" title="Steven Pinker">Steven Pinker</a>, to whom Donald and Deacon can be contrasted.
</p><p>As reason is symbolic thinking, and peculiarly human, then this implies that humans have a special ability to maintain a clear consciousness of the distinctness of "icons" or images and the real things they represent. Starting with a modern author, Merlin Donald writes<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup>
</p>
<blockquote><p>A dog might perceive the "meaning" of a fight that was realistically play-acted by humans, but it could not reconstruct the message or distinguish the representation from its referent (a real fight). [...] Trained apes are able to make this distinction; young children make this distinction early – hence, their effortless distinction between play-acting an event and the event itself</p></blockquote>
<p>In classical descriptions, an equivalent description of this mental faculty is <i>eikasia</i>, in the philosophy of Plato.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup> This is the ability to perceive whether a perception is an image of something else, related somehow but not the same, and therefore allows humans to perceive that a dream or memory or a reflection in a mirror is not reality as such. What Klein refers to as <i>dianoetic eikasia</i> is the <i>eikasia</i> concerned specifically with thinking and mental images, such as those mental symbols, icons, <i>signes</i>, and marks discussed above as definitive of reason. Explaining reason from this direction: human thinking is special in the way that we often understand visible things as if they were themselves images of our intelligible "objects of thought" as "foundations" (<i>hypothēses</i> in Ancient Greek). This thinking (<i>dianoia</i>) is "...an activity which consists in making the vast and diffuse jungle of the visible world depend on a plurality of more 'precise' <i>noēta</i>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup>
</p><p>Both Merlin Donald and the Socratic authors such as Plato and Aristotle emphasize the importance of <i>mimesis</i>, often translated as <i>imitation</i> or <i>representation</i>. Donald writes<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup>
</p>
<blockquote><p>Imitation is found especially in monkeys and apes [... but ...] Mimesis is fundamentally different from imitation and mimicry in that it involves the invention of intentional representations. [...] Mimesis is not absolutely tied to external communication.</p></blockquote>
<p><i>Mimēsis</i> is a concept, now popular again in academic discussion, that was particularly prevalent in Plato's works, and within Aristotle, it is discussed mainly in the <i>Poetics</i>. In Michael Davis's account of the theory of man in this work.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup>
</p>
<blockquote><p>It is the distinctive feature of human action, that whenever we choose what we do, we imagine an action for ourselves as though we were inspecting it from the outside. Intentions are nothing more than imagined actions, internalizings of the external. All action is therefore imitation of action; it is poetic...<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<p>Donald like Plato (and Aristotle, especially in <i>On Memory and Recollection</i>), emphasizes the peculiarity in humans of voluntary initiation of a search through one's mental world. The ancient Greek <i>anamnēsis</i>, normally translated as "recollection" was opposed to <i>mneme</i> or <i>memory</i>. Memory, shared with some animals,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup> requires a consciousness not only of what happened in the past, but also <i>that</i> something happened in the past, which is in other words a kind of <i>eikasia</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup> "...but nothing except man is able to recollect."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup> Recollection is a deliberate effort to search for and recapture something once known. Klein writes that, "To become aware of our having forgotten something means to begin recollecting."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup> Donald calls the same thing <i>autocueing</i>, which he explains as follows:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup> "Mimetic acts are reproducible on the basis of internal, self-generated cues. This permits voluntary recall of mimetic representations, without the aid of external cues – probably the earliest form of representational <i>thinking</i>."
</p><p>In a celebrated paper in modern times, the fantasy author and philologist <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/J.R.R._Tolkien" title="J.R.R. Tolkien">J.R.R. Tolkien</a> wrote in his essay "On Fairy Stories" that the terms "fantasy" and "enchantment" are connected to not only "....the satisfaction of certain primordial human desires...." but also "...the origin of language and of the mind".
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Logical_reasoning_methods_and_argumentation">Logical reasoning methods and argumentation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Reason&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Logical reasoning methods and argumentation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>A subdivision of <a href="/wiki/Philosophy" title="Philosophy">philosophy</a> is <a href="/wiki/Logic" title="Logic">logic</a>. Logic is the study of reasoning. Looking at logical categorizations of different types of reasoning, the traditional main division made in philosophy is between <a href="/wiki/Deductive_reasoning" title="Deductive reasoning">deductive reasoning</a> and <a href="/wiki/Inductive_reasoning" title="Inductive reasoning">inductive reasoning</a>. <a href="/wiki/Logic" title="Logic">Formal logic</a> has been described as <i>the science of deduction</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup> The study of inductive reasoning is generally carried out within the field known as <a href="/wiki/Informal_logic" title="Informal logic">informal logic</a> or <a href="/wiki/Critical_thinking" title="Critical thinking">critical thinking</a>.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Deductive_reasoning">Deductive reasoning</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Reason&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Deductive reasoning">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Deductive_reasoning" title="Deductive reasoning">Deductive reasoning</a></div>
<p>Deduction is a form of reasoning in which a conclusion follows necessarily from the stated premises. A deduction is also the conclusion reached by a deductive reasoning process. One classic example of deductive reasoning is that found in <a href="/wiki/Syllogism" title="Syllogism">syllogisms</a> like the following:
</p>
<ul><li>Premise 1: All humans are mortal.</li>
<li>Premise 2: Socrates is a human.</li>
<li>Conclusion: Socrates is mortal.</li></ul>
<p>The reasoning in this argument is deductively <a href="/wiki/Validity_(logic)" title="Validity (logic)">valid</a> because there is no way in which the premises, 1 and 2, could be true and the conclusion, 3, be false.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Inductive_reasoning">Inductive reasoning</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Reason&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Inductive reasoning">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Inductive_reasoning" title="Inductive reasoning">Inductive reasoning</a></div>
<p>Induction is a form of inference producing propositions about unobserved objects or types, either specifically or generally, based on previous observation. It is used to ascribe <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Category_of_being" title="Category of being">properties or relations</a> to objects or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Type_(metaphysics)" title="Type (metaphysics)">types</a> based on <a href="/wiki/Event_(philosophy)" title="Event (philosophy)">previous observations or experiences</a>, or to formulate general statements or <a href="/wiki/Law_(principle)" title="Law (principle)">laws</a> based on limited observations of recurring <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Phenomena" title="Phenomena">phenomenal</a> patterns.
</p><p>Inductive reasoning contrasts strongly with deductive reasoning in that, even in the best, or strongest, cases of inductive reasoning, the truth of the premises does not guarantee the truth of the conclusion. Instead, the conclusion of an inductive argument follows with some degree of <a href="/wiki/Probability" title="Probability">probability</a>. Relatedly, the conclusion of an inductive argument contains more information than is already contained in the premises. Thus, this method of reasoning is ampliative.
</p><p>A classic example of inductive reasoning comes from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Empiricist" title="Empiricist">empiricist</a> <a href="/wiki/David_Hume" title="David Hume">David Hume</a>:
</p>
<ul><li>Premise: The sun has risen in the east every morning up until now.</li>
<li>Conclusion: The sun will also rise in the east tomorrow.</li></ul>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Analogical_reasoning">Analogical reasoning</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Reason&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Analogical reasoning">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Analogical_reasoning" title="Analogical reasoning">Analogical reasoning</a></div>
<p>Analogical reasoning is a form of inductive reasoning from a particular to a particular. It is often used in <a href="/wiki/Case-based_reasoning" title="Case-based reasoning">case-based reasoning</a>, especially legal reasoning.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup> An example follows:
</p>
<ul><li>Premise 1: Socrates is human and mortal.</li>
<li>Premise 2: Plato is human.</li>
<li>Conclusion: Plato is mortal.</li></ul>
<p>Analogical reasoning is a weaker form of inductive reasoning from a single example, because inductive reasoning typically uses a large number of examples to reason from the particular to the general.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup> Analogical reasoning often leads to wrong conclusions. For example:
</p>
<ul><li>Premise 1: Socrates is human and male.</li>
<li>Premise 2: <a href="/wiki/Ada_Lovelace" title="Ada Lovelace">Ada Lovelace</a> is human.</li>
<li>Conclusion: Ada Lovelace is male.</li></ul>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Abductive_reasoning">Abductive reasoning</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Reason&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Abductive reasoning">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Abductive_reasoning" title="Abductive reasoning">Abductive reasoning</a></div>
<p>Abductive reasoning, or argument to the best explanation, is a form of reasoning that doesn't fit in deductive or inductive, since it starts with incomplete set of observations and proceeds with likely possible explanations so the conclusion in an abductive argument does not follow with certainty from its premises and concerns something unobserved. What distinguishes abduction from the other forms of reasoning is an attempt to favour one conclusion above others, by subjective judgement or attempting to falsify alternative explanations or by demonstrating the likelihood of the favoured conclusion, given a set of more or less disputable assumptions. For example, when a patient displays certain symptoms, there might be various possible causes, but one of these is preferred above others as being more probable.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Fallacious_reasoning">Fallacious reasoning</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Reason&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Fallacious reasoning">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Fallacy" title="Fallacy">Fallacy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Formal_fallacy" title="Formal fallacy">Formal fallacy</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Informal_fallacy" title="Informal fallacy">Informal fallacy</a></div>
<p>Flawed reasoning in arguments is known as <a href="/wiki/Fallacy" title="Fallacy">fallacious reasoning</a>. Bad reasoning within arguments can be because it commits either a <a href="/wiki/Formal_fallacy" title="Formal fallacy">formal fallacy</a> or an <a href="/wiki/Informal_fallacy" title="Informal fallacy">informal fallacy</a>.
</p><p>Formal fallacies occur when there is a problem with the form, or structure, of the argument. The word "formal" refers to this link to the <i>form</i> of the argument. An argument that contains a formal fallacy will always be invalid.
</p><p>An informal fallacy is an error in reasoning that occurs due to a problem with the <i>content</i>, rather than mere <i>structure</i>, of the argument.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Traditional_problems_raised_concerning_reason">Traditional problems raised concerning reason</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Reason&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Traditional problems raised concerning reason">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Philosophy is sometimes described as a life of reason, with normal human reason pursued in a more consistent and dedicated way than usual. Two categories of problem concerning reason have long been discussed by philosophers concerning reason, essentially being reasonings about reasoning itself as a human aim, or philosophizing about philosophizing. The first question is concerning whether we can be confident that reason can achieve <a href="/wiki/Knowledge" title="Knowledge">knowledge</a> of <a href="/wiki/Truth" title="Truth">truth</a> better than other ways of trying to achieve such knowledge. The other question is whether a life of reason, a life that aims to be guided by reason, can be expected to achieve a <a href="/wiki/Eudaimonia" title="Eudaimonia">happy life</a> more so than other ways of life (whether such a life of reason results in knowledge or not).
</p>
<h3><span id="Reason_versus_truth.2C_and_.22first_principles.22"></span><span class="mw-headline" id='Reason_versus_truth,_and_"first_principles"'>Reason versus truth, and "first principles"</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Reason&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title='Edit section: Reason versus truth, and "first principles"'>edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Truth" title="Truth">Truth</a>, <a href="/wiki/First_principle" title="First principle">First principle</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Nous" title="Nous">Nous</a></div>
<p>Since <a href="/wiki/Classical_antiquity" title="Classical antiquity">classical</a> times a question has remained constant in philosophical debate (which is sometimes seen as a conflict between movements called <a href="/wiki/Platonism" title="Platonism">Platonism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Aristotelianism" title="Aristotelianism">Aristotelianism</a>) concerning the role of reason in confirming <a href="/wiki/Truth" title="Truth">truth</a>. People use logic, <a href="/wiki/Deductive_reasoning" title="Deductive reasoning">deduction</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Inductive_reasoning" title="Inductive reasoning">induction</a>, to reach conclusions they think are true. Conclusions reached in this way are considered, according to Aristotle, more certain than sense perceptions on their own.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup> On the other hand, if such reasoned conclusions are only built originally upon a foundation of sense perceptions, then, our most logical conclusions can never be said to be certain because they are built upon the very same fallible perceptions they seek to better.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup>
</p><p>This leads to the question of what types of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/First_principles" title="First principles">first principles</a>, or starting points of reasoning, are available for someone seeking to come to true conclusions. In Greek, "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/First_principles" title="First principles">first principles</a>" are <i><a href="/wiki/Arche" title="Arche">archai</a></i>, "starting points",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup> and the faculty used to perceive them is sometimes referred to in Aristotle<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup> and Plato<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup> as <i><a href="/wiki/Nous" title="Nous">nous</a></i> which was close in meaning to <i>awareness</i> or <i><a href="/wiki/Consciousness" title="Consciousness">consciousness</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Empiricism" title="Empiricism">Empiricism</a> (sometimes associated with Aristotle<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup> but more correctly associated with <a href="/wiki/Great_Britain" title="Great Britain">British</a> philosophers such as <a href="/wiki/John_Locke" title="John Locke">John Locke</a> and <a href="/wiki/David_Hume" title="David Hume">David Hume</a>, as well as their ancient equivalents such as <a href="/wiki/Democritus" title="Democritus">Democritus</a>) asserts that sensory impressions are the only available starting points for reasoning and attempting to attain truth. This approach always leads to the controversial conclusion that <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Absolute_knowledge" title="Absolute knowledge">absolute knowledge</a> is not attainable. <a href="/wiki/Idealism" title="Idealism">Idealism</a>, (associated with Plato and his school), claims that there is a "higher" reality, from which certain people can directly arrive at truth without needing to rely only upon the senses, and that this higher reality is therefore the primary source of truth.
</p><p>Philosophers such as <a href="/wiki/Plato" title="Plato">Plato</a>, <a href="/wiki/Aristotle" title="Aristotle">Aristotle</a>, <a href="/wiki/Al-Farabi" title="Al-Farabi">Al-Farabi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Avicenna" title="Avicenna">Avicenna</a>, <a href="/wiki/Averroes" title="Averroes">Averroes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Maimonides" title="Maimonides">Maimonides</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aquinas" title="Aquinas">Aquinas</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hegel" title="Hegel">Hegel</a> are sometimes said to have argued that reason must be fixed and discoverable—perhaps by dialectic, analysis, or study. In the vision of these thinkers, reason is divine or at least has divine attributes. Such an approach allowed religious philosophers such as <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Aquinas" title="Thomas Aquinas">Thomas Aquinas</a> and <a href="/wiki/%C3%89tienne_Gilson" title="Étienne Gilson">Étienne Gilson</a> to try to show that reason and <a href="/wiki/Revelation" title="Revelation">revelation</a> are compatible. According to Hegel, "...the only thought which Philosophy brings with it to the contemplation of <a href="/wiki/History" title="History">History</a>, is the simple conception of reason; that reason is the Sovereign of the World; that the history of the world, therefore, presents us with a rational process."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup>
</p><p>Since the 17th century <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rationalist" title="Rationalist">rationalists</a>, reason has often been taken to be a <a href="/wiki/Subjectivity" title="Subjectivity">subjective faculty</a>, or rather the unaided ability (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pure_reason" title="Pure reason">pure reason</a>) to form concepts. For <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Descartes" title="Descartes">Descartes</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spinoza" title="Spinoza">Spinoza</a> and <a href="/wiki/Gottfried_Wilhelm_Leibniz" title="Gottfried Wilhelm Leibniz">Leibniz</a>, this was associated with <a href="/wiki/Mathematics" title="Mathematics">mathematics</a>. <a href="/wiki/Immanuel_Kant" title="Immanuel Kant">Kant</a> attempted to show that pure reason could form concepts (<a href="/wiki/Time" title="Time">time</a> and <a href="/wiki/Space" title="Space">space</a>) that are the conditions of experience. Kant made his argument in opposition to Hume, who denied that reason had any role to play in experience.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Reason_versus_emotion_or_passion">Reason versus emotion or passion</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Reason&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Reason versus emotion or passion">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Emotion" title="Emotion">Emotion</a> and <a href="/wiki/Passion_(emotion)" title="Passion (emotion)">Passion (emotion)</a></div>
<p>After Plato and Aristotle, <a href="/wiki/Western_literature" title="Western literature">western literature</a> often treated reason as being the faculty that trained the passions and appetites.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Stoic_philosophy" title="Stoic philosophy">Stoic philosophy</a>, by contrast, claimed most emotions were merely false judgements.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-80">[80]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-:1-81">[81]</a></sup> According to the stoics the only good is virtue, and the only evil is vice, therefore emotions that judged things other than vice to be bad (such as fear or distress), or things other than virtue to be good (such as greed) were simply false judgements and should be discarded (though positive emotions based on true judgements, such as kindness, were acceptable).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_80-1"><a href="#cite_note-:0-80">[80]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_81-1"><a href="#cite_note-:1-81">[81]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup> After the critiques of reason in the early Enlightenment the appetites were rarely discussed or conflated with the passions.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Some Enlightenment camps took after the Stoics to say Reason should oppose Passion rather than order it, while others like the Romantics believed that Passion displaces Reason, as in the maxim "follow your heart".<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>Reason has been seen as cold, an "enemy of mystery and ambiguity",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Radford_and_Frazier_(2017)_83-0"><a href="#cite_note-Radford_and_Frazier_(2017)-83">[83]</a></sup> a slave, or judge, of the passions, notably in the work of <a href="/wiki/David_Hume" title="David Hume">David Hume</a>, and more recently of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Freud" title="Freud">Freud</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Reasoning which claims that the object of a desire is demanded by logic alone is called <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rationalization_(making_excuses)" title="Rationalization (making excuses)">rationalization</a></i>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rousseau" title="Rousseau">Rousseau</a> first proposed, in his second <i><a href="/wiki/Discourse_on_Inequality" title="Discourse on Inequality">Discourse</a></i>, that reason and political life is not natural and possibly harmful to mankind.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup> He asked what really can be said about what is natural to mankind. What, other than reason and civil society, "best suits his constitution"? Rousseau saw "two principles prior to reason" in human nature. First we hold an intense interest in our own well-being. Secondly we object to the suffering or death of any sentient being, especially one like ourselves.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup> These two passions lead us to desire more than we could achieve. We become dependent upon each other, and on relationships of authority and obedience. This effectively puts the human race into slavery. Rousseau says that he almost dares to assert that nature does not destine men to be healthy. According to <a href="/wiki/Richard_Velkley" title="Richard Velkley">Richard Velkley</a>, "Rousseau outlines certain programs of rational self-correction, most notably the political legislation of the <i><a href="/wiki/The_Social_Contract" title="The Social Contract">Contrat Social</a></i> and the moral education in <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Emile:_or,_On_Education" title="Emile: or, On Education">Émile</a></i>. All the same, Rousseau understands such corrections to be only ameliorations of an essentially unsatisfactory condition, that of socially and intellectually corrupted humanity."
</p><p>This quandary presented by Rousseau led to <a href="/wiki/Immanuel_Kant" title="Immanuel Kant">Kant</a>'s new way of justifying reason as freedom to create good and evil. These therefore are not to be blamed on nature or God.
