In various ways, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/German_Idealism" title="German Idealism">German Idealism</a> after Kant, and major later figures such <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nietzsche" title="Nietzsche">Nietzsche</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bergson" title="Bergson">Bergson</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Husserl" title="Husserl">Husserl</a>, <a href="/wiki/Max_Scheler" title="Max Scheler">Scheler</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Heidegger" title="Heidegger">Heidegger</a>, remain preoccupied with problems coming from the metaphysical demands or <i>urges</i> of <i>reason</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup> The influence of Rousseau and these later writers is also large upon art and politics. Many writers (such as <a href="/wiki/Nikos_Kazantzakis" title="Nikos Kazantzakis">Nikos Kazantzakis</a>) extol passion and disparage reason. In politics modern <a href="/wiki/Nationalism" title="Nationalism">nationalism</a> comes from Rousseau's argument that rationalist <a href="/wiki/Cosmopolitanism" title="Cosmopolitanism">cosmopolitanism</a> brings man ever further from his natural state.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup>
</p><p>Another view on reason and emotion was proposed in the 1994 book titled <i><a href="/wiki/Descartes%27_Error" title="Descartes' Error">Descartes' Error</a></i> by <a href="/wiki/Antonio_Damasio" title="Antonio Damasio">Antonio Damasio</a>. In it, Damasio presents the "<a href="/wiki/Somatic_marker_hypothesis" title="Somatic marker hypothesis">Somatic Marker Hypothesis</a>" which states that emotions guide behavior and decision-making. Damasio argues that these somatic markers (known collectively as "gut feelings") are "intuitive signals" that direct our decision making processes in a certain way that cannot be solved with rationality alone. Damasio further argues that rationality requires emotional input in order to function.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Reason_versus_faith_or_tradition">Reason versus faith or tradition</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Reason&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Reason versus faith or tradition">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Faith" title="Faith">Faith</a>, <a href="/wiki/Religion" title="Religion">Religion</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Tradition" title="Tradition">Tradition</a></div>
<p>There are many religious traditions, some of which are explicitly <a href="/wiki/Fideism" title="Fideism">fideist</a> and others of which claim varying degrees of <a href="/wiki/Rationalism" title="Rationalism">rationalism</a>. Secular critics sometimes accuse all religious adherents of irrationality, since they claim such adherents are guilty of ignoring, suppressing, or forbidding some kinds of reasoning concerning some subjects (such as religious dogmas, moral taboos, etc.).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup> Though the <a href="/wiki/Theology" title="Theology">theologies</a> and <a href="/wiki/Religion" title="Religion">religions</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Monotheism" title="Monotheism">classical monotheism</a> typically do not admit to be <a href="/wiki/Irrationality" title="Irrationality">irrational</a>, there is often a perceived conflict or tension between <a href="/wiki/Faith" title="Faith">faith</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tradition" title="Tradition">tradition</a> on the one hand, and reason on the other, as potentially competing sources of <a href="/wiki/Wisdom" title="Wisdom">wisdom</a>, <a href="/wiki/Law" title="Law">law</a> and <a href="/wiki/Truth" title="Truth">truth</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup>
</p><p>Religious adherents sometimes respond by arguing that faith and reason can be reconciled, or have different non-overlapping domains, or that critics engage in a similar kind of irrationalism:
</p>
<ul><li><i>Reconciliation</i>: Philosopher <a href="/wiki/Alvin_Plantinga" title="Alvin Plantinga">Alvin Plantinga</a> argues that there is no real conflict between reason and classical theism because classical theism explains (among other things) why the universe is intelligible and why reason can successfully grasp it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup></li>
<li><i>Non-overlapping magisteria</i>: Evolutionary biologist <a href="/wiki/Stephen_Jay_Gould" title="Stephen Jay Gould">Stephen Jay Gould</a> argues that there need not be conflict between reason and religious belief because they are each authoritative in their own domain (or "magisterium").<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup> If so, reason can work on those problems over which it has authority while other sources of knowledge or opinion can have authority on the big questions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup></li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Tu_quoque" title="Tu quoque">Tu quoque</a></i>: Philosophers <a href="/wiki/Alasdair_MacIntyre" title="Alasdair MacIntyre">Alasdair MacIntyre</a> and <a href="/wiki/Charles_Taylor_(philosopher)" title="Charles Taylor (philosopher)">Charles Taylor</a> argue that those critics of traditional religion who are adherents of <a href="/wiki/Secularity" title="Secularity">secular</a> <a href="/wiki/Liberalism" title="Liberalism">liberalism</a> are also sometimes guilty of ignoring, suppressing, and forbidding some kinds of reasoning about subjects.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup> Similarly, philosophers of science such as <a href="/wiki/Paul_Feyerabend" title="Paul Feyerabend">Paul Feyarabend</a> argue that scientists sometimes ignore or suppress evidence contrary to the dominant <a href="/wiki/Paradigm" title="Paradigm">paradigm</a>.</li>
<li><i>Unification</i>: Theologian Joseph Ratzinger, later <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Benedict_XVI" title="Benedict XVI">Benedict XVI</a>, asserted that "Christianity has understood itself as the religion of the Logos, as the religion according to reason," referring to John 1:Ἐν ἀρχῇ ἦν ὁ λόγος, usually translated as "In the beginning was the Word (Logos)." Thus, he said that the Christian faith is "open to all that is truly rational", and that the rationality of Western Enlightenment "is of Christian origin".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup></li></ul>
<p>Some commentators have claimed that <a href="/wiki/Western_culture" title="Western culture">Western civilization</a> can be almost defined by its serious testing of the limits of tension between "unaided" reason and <a href="/wiki/Faith" title="Faith">faith</a> in "<a href="/wiki/Revelation" title="Revelation">revealed</a>" truths—figuratively summarized as <a href="/wiki/Athens" title="Athens">Athens</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jerusalem" title="Jerusalem">Jerusalem</a>, respectively.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Leo_Strauss" title="Leo Strauss">Leo Strauss</a> spoke of a "Greater <a href="/wiki/Western_world" title="Western world">West</a>" that included all areas under the influence of the tension between Greek rationalism and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Abrahamic" title="Abrahamic">Abrahamic</a> revelation, including the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Muslim" title="Muslim">Muslim</a> lands. He was particularly influenced by the great <a href="/wiki/Islamic_philosophy" title="Islamic philosophy">Muslim philosopher</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Farabi" title="Farabi">Al-Farabi</a>. To consider to what extent <a href="/wiki/Eastern_philosophy" title="Eastern philosophy">Eastern philosophy</a> might have partaken of these important tensions, Strauss thought it best to consider whether <a href="/wiki/Dharma" title="Dharma">dharma</a> or <a href="/wiki/Tao" title="Tao">tao</a> may be equivalent to <a href="/wiki/Nature_(philosophy)" title="Nature (philosophy)">Nature</a> (by which we mean <i><a href="/wiki/Physis" title="Physis">physis</a></i> in Greek). According to Strauss the beginning of philosophy involved the "discovery or invention of nature" and the "pre-philosophical equivalent of nature" was supplied by "such notions as 'custom' or 'ways<span style="padding-right:.15em;">'</span>", which appear to be <i>really universal in all times and places</i>. The philosophical concept of nature or natures as a way of understanding <i>archai</i> (first principles of knowledge) brought about a peculiar tension between reasoning on the one hand, and tradition or faith on the other.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup>
</p><p>Although there is this special history of debate concerning reason and faith in the Islamic, Christian and Jewish traditions, the pursuit of reason is sometimes argued to be compatible with the other practice of other religions of a different nature, such as <a href="/wiki/Hinduism" title="Hinduism">Hinduism</a>, because they do not define their tenets in such an absolute way.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Reason_in_particular_fields_of_study">Reason in particular fields of study</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Reason&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Reason in particular fields of study">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Psychology_and_cognitive_science">Psychology and cognitive science</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Reason&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Psychology and cognitive science">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Psychology_of_reasoning" title="Psychology of reasoning">Psychology of reasoning</a></div>
<p>Scientific research into reasoning is carried out within the fields of <a href="/wiki/Psychology" title="Psychology">psychology</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cognitive_science" title="Cognitive science">cognitive science</a>. Psychologists attempt to determine whether or not people are capable of rational thought in a number of different circumstances.
</p><p>Assessing how well someone engages in reasoning is the project of determining the extent to which the person is <a href="/wiki/Rationality" title="Rationality">rational</a> or acts rationally. It is a key research question in the <a href="/wiki/Psychology_of_reasoning" title="Psychology of reasoning">psychology of reasoning</a> and cognitive science of reasoning. <a href="/wiki/Rationality" title="Rationality">Rationality</a> is often divided into its respective <a href="/wiki/Rationality#Theoretical_and_practical_rationality" title="Rationality">theoretical and practical counterparts</a>.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Behavioral_experiments_on_human_reasoning">Behavioral experiments on human reasoning</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Reason&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Behavioral experiments on human reasoning">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Experimental cognitive psychologists carry out research on reasoning behaviour. Such research may focus, for example, on how people perform on tests of reasoning such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Intelligence_tests" title="Intelligence tests">intelligence</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/IQ" title="IQ">IQ</a> tests, or on how well people's reasoning matches ideals set by logic (see, for example, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wason_test" title="Wason test">Wason test</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup> Experiments examine how people make inferences from conditionals e.g., <i>If A then B</i> and how they make inferences about alternatives, e.g., <i>A or else B</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup> They test whether people can make valid deductions about spatial and temporal relations, e.g., <i>A is to the left of B</i>, or <i>A happens after B</i>, and about quantified assertions, e.g., <i>All the A are B</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup> Experiments investigate how people make inferences about factual situations, hypothetical possibilities, probabilities, and <a href="/wiki/Counterfactual_thinking" title="Counterfactual thinking">counterfactual</a> situations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span id="Developmental_studies_of_children.27s_reasoning"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Developmental_studies_of_children's_reasoning">Developmental studies of children's reasoning</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Reason&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Developmental studies of children's reasoning">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Developmental psychologists investigate the development of reasoning from birth to adulthood. Piaget's <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Theory_of_cognitive_development" title="Theory of cognitive development">theory of cognitive development</a> was the first complete theory of reasoning development. Subsequently, several alternative theories were proposed, including the <a href="/wiki/Neo-Piagetian_theories_of_cognitive_development" title="Neo-Piagetian theories of cognitive development">neo-Piagetian theories of cognitive development</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[107]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Neuroscience_of_reasoning">Neuroscience of reasoning</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Reason&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Neuroscience of reasoning">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The biological functioning of the brain is studied by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Neurophysiologist" title="Neurophysiologist">neurophysiologists</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cognitive_neuroscience" title="Cognitive neuroscience">cognitive neuroscientists</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Neuropsychologist" title="Neuropsychologist">neuropsychologists</a>. Research in this area includes research into the structure and function of normally functioning brains, and of damaged or otherwise unusual brains. In addition to carrying out research into reasoning, some psychologists, for example, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Clinical_psychologist" title="Clinical psychologist">clinical psychologists</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Psychotherapists" title="Psychotherapists">psychotherapists</a> work to alter people's reasoning habits when they are unhelpful.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Computer_science">Computer science</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Reason&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Computer science">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Automated_reasoning">Automated reasoning</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Reason&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Automated reasoning">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Automated_reasoning" title="Automated reasoning">Automated reasoning</a> and <a href="/wiki/Computational_logic" title="Computational logic">Computational logic</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Reasoning_system" title="Reasoning system">Reasoning system</a>, <a href="/wiki/Case-based_reasoning" title="Case-based reasoning">Case-based reasoning</a>, <a href="/wiki/Semantic_reasoner" title="Semantic reasoner">Semantic reasoner</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Knowledge_reasoning" title="Knowledge reasoning">Knowledge reasoning</a></div>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Artificial_intelligence" title="Artificial intelligence">artificial intelligence</a> and <a href="/wiki/Computer_science" title="Computer science">computer science</a>, scientists study and use <a href="/wiki/Automated_reasoning" title="Automated reasoning">automated reasoning</a> for diverse applications including <a href="/wiki/Automated_theorem_proving" title="Automated theorem proving">automated theorem proving</a> the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Formal_semantics_of_programming_languages" title="Formal semantics of programming languages">formal semantics of programming languages</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Formal_specification" title="Formal specification">formal specification</a> in <a href="/wiki/Software_engineering" title="Software engineering">software engineering</a>.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Meta-reasoning">Meta-reasoning</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Reason&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Meta-reasoning">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Metacognition" title="Metacognition">Metacognition</a></div>
<p><b>Meta-reasoning</b> is reasoning about reasoning. In computer science, a system performs meta-reasoning when it is reasoning about its own operation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[108]</a></sup> This requires a programming language capable of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Reflection_(computer_science)" title="Reflection (computer science)">reflection</a>, the ability to observe and modify its own structure and behaviour.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Evolution_of_reason">Evolution of reason</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Reason&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Evolution of reason">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>

ambox-content" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg"><img alt="[icon]" data-file-height="31" data-file-width="44" You can help by <a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Reason&amp;action=edit&amp;section=">adding to it</a>.  <span class="date-container"><i>(<span class="date">August 2017</span>)</i></span></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Capuchin_monkeys_sharing.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1212" data-file-width="1000" decoding="async" height="182" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f8/Capuchin_monkeys_sharing.jpg/150px-Capuchin_monkeys_sharing.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f8/Capuchin_monkeys_sharing.jpg/225px-Capuchin_monkeys_sharing.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f8/Capuchin_monkeys_sharing.jpg/300px-Capuchin_monkeys_sharing.jpg 2x" width="150"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Capuchin_monkeys_sharing.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Dan Sperber believes that reasoning in groups is more effective and promotes their evolutionary fitness.</div></div></div>
<p>A species could benefit greatly from better abilities to reason about, predict and understand the world. French social and cognitive scientists <a href="/wiki/Dan_Sperber" title="Dan Sperber">Dan Sperber</a> and Hugo Mercier argue that there could have been other forces driving the evolution of reason. They point out that reasoning is very difficult for humans to do effectively, and that it is hard for individuals to doubt their own beliefs (<a href="/wiki/Confirmation_bias" title="Confirmation bias">confirmation bias</a>). Reasoning is most effective when it is done as a collective – as demonstrated by the success of projects like <a href="/wiki/Science" title="Science">science</a>. They suggest that there are not just individual, but <a href="/wiki/Group_selection" title="Group selection">group selection</a> pressures at play. Any group that managed to find ways of reasoning effectively would reap benefits for all its members, increasing their <a href="/wiki/Fitness_(biology)" title="Fitness (biology)">fitness</a>. This could also help explain why humans, according to Sperber, are not optimized to reason effectively alone. Their argumentative theory of reasoning claims that reason may have more to do with winning arguments than with the search for the truth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[109]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[110]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Reason_in_political_philosophy_and_ethics">Reason in political philosophy and ethics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Reason&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Reason in political philosophy and ethics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Political_Philosophy" title="Political Philosophy">Political Philosophy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ethics" title="Ethics">Ethics</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Good" title="The Good">The Good</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Aristotle" title="Aristotle">Aristotle</a> famously described reason (with language) as a part of <a href="/wiki/Human_nature" title="Human nature">human nature</a>, which means that it is best for humans to live "politically" meaning in communities of about the size and type of a small <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/City_state" title="City state">city state</a> (<i>polis</i> in Greek). For example...
</p>
<blockquote><p>It is clear, then, that a human being is more of a political [<i>politikon</i> = of the <i>polis</i>] animal [<i>zōion</i>] than is any bee or than any of those animals that live in herds. For nature, as we say, makes nothing in vain, and humans are the only animals who possess reasoned speech [<i>logos</i>]. Voice, of course, serves to indicate what is painful and pleasant; that is why it is also found in other animals, because their nature has reached the point where they can perceive what is painful and pleasant and express these to each other. But speech [<i>logos</i>] serves to make plain what is advantageous and harmful and so also what is just and unjust. For it is a peculiarity of humans, in contrast to the other animals, to have perception of good and bad, just and unjust, and the like; and the community in these things makes a household or city [<i>polis</i>]. [...] By nature, then, the drive for such a community exists in everyone, but the first to set one up is responsible for things of very great goodness. For as humans are the best of all animals when perfected, so they are the worst when divorced from law and right. The reason is that injustice is most difficult to deal with when furnished with weapons, and the weapons a human being has are meant by nature to go along with prudence and virtue, but it is only too possible to turn them to contrary uses. Consequently, if a human being lacks virtue, he is the most unholy and savage thing, and when it comes to sex and food, the worst. But justice is something political [to do with the <i>polis</i>], for right is the arrangement of the political community, and right is discrimination of what is just. (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aristotle%27s_Politics" title="Aristotle's Politics">Aristotle's Politics</a> 1253a 1.2. Peter Simpson's translation, with Greek terms inserted in square brackets.)</p></blockquote>
<p>The concept of human nature being fixed in this way, implied, in other words, that we can define what type of community is always best for people. This argument has remained a central argument in all political, ethical and moral thinking since then, and has become especially controversial since firstly <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rousseau" title="Rousseau">Rousseau</a>'s Second Discourse, and secondly, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Theory_of_Evolution" title="Theory of Evolution">Theory of Evolution</a>. Already in Aristotle there was an awareness that the <i>polis</i> had not always existed and had needed to be invented or developed by humans themselves. The household came first, and the first villages and cities were just extensions of that, with the first cities being run as if they were still families with Kings acting like fathers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[111]</a></sup>
</p>
<blockquote><p><a href="/wiki/Friendship" title="Friendship">Friendship</a> [<i>philia</i>] seems to prevail [in] man and woman according to <a href="/wiki/Nature" title="Nature">nature</a> [<i>kata phusin</i>]; for people are by nature [<i>tēi phusei</i>] pairing [<i>sunduastikon</i>] more than political [<i>politikon</i> = of the <i>polis</i>], in as much as the household [<i>oikos</i>] is prior [<i>proteron</i> = earlier] and more necessary than the <i>polis</i> and making children is more common [<i>koinoteron</i>] with the animals. In the other animals, community [<i>koinōnia</i>] goes no further than this, but people live together [<i>sumoikousin</i>] not only for the sake of making children, but also for the things for life; for from the start the functions [<i>erga</i>] are divided, and are different [for] man and woman. Thus they supply each other, putting their own into the common [<i>eis to koinon</i>]. It is for these [reasons] that both utility [<i>chrēsimon</i>] and pleasure [<i>hēdu</i>] seem to be found in this kind of friendship. (<a href="/wiki/Nicomachean_Ethics" title="Nicomachean Ethics">Nicomachean Ethics</a>, VIII.12.1162a. Rough literal translation with Greek terms shown in square brackets.)</p></blockquote>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rousseau" title="Rousseau">Rousseau</a> in his Second Discourse finally took the shocking step of claiming that this traditional account has things in reverse: with reason, language and rationally organized communities all having developed over a long period of time merely as a result of the fact that some habits of cooperation were found to solve certain types of problems, and that once such cooperation became more important, it forced people to develop increasingly complex cooperation—often only to defend themselves from each other.
</p><p>In other words, according to Rousseau, reason, language and rational community did not arise because of any conscious decision or plan by humans or gods, nor because of any pre-existing human nature. As a result, he claimed, living together in rationally organized communities like modern humans is a development with many negative aspects compared to the original state of man as an ape. If anything is specifically human in this theory, it is the flexibility and adaptability of humans. This view of the animal origins of distinctive human characteristics later received support from <a href="/wiki/Charles_Darwin" title="Charles Darwin">Charles Darwin</a>'s <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Theory_of_Evolution" title="Theory of Evolution">Theory of Evolution</a>.
</p><p>The two competing theories concerning the origins of reason are relevant to political and ethical thought because, according to the Aristotelian theory, a best way of living together exists independently of historical circumstances. According to Rousseau, we should even doubt that reason, language and politics are a good thing, as opposed to being simply the best option given the particular course of events that lead to today. Rousseau's theory, that human nature is malleable rather than fixed, is often taken to imply, for example by <a href="/wiki/Karl_Marx" title="Karl Marx">Karl Marx</a>, a wider range of possible ways of living together than traditionally known.
</p><p>However, while Rousseau's initial impact encouraged bloody revolutions against traditional politics, including both the <a href="/wiki/French_Revolution" title="French Revolution">French Revolution</a> and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Russian_Revolution_(1917)" title="Russian Revolution (1917)">Russian Revolution</a>, his own conclusions about the best forms of community seem to have been remarkably classical, in favor of city-states such as <a href="/wiki/Geneva" title="Geneva">Geneva</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Arcadia_(utopia)" title="Arcadia (utopia)">rural living</a>.
</p>

<li id="cite_note-23"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-23">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation web cs1" id="CITEREFDreyfus">Dreyfus, Hubert. <a class="external text" href="http://socrates.berkeley.edu/~hdreyfus/rtf/Limits_of_Telepresence_6_99.rtf" rel="nofollow">"Telepistemology: Descartes' Last Stand"</a>. socrates.berkeley.edu<span class="reference-accessdate">. <li id="cite_note-25"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-25">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation cs2" id="CITEREFHobbes1839">Hobbes, Thomas (1839),  Molesworth (ed.), <a class="external text" href="https://archive.org/details/englishworkstho21hobbgoog" rel="nofollow"><i>De Corpore</i></a>, London, J. Bohn</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=De+Corpore&amp;rft.pub=London%2C+J.+Bohn&amp;rft.date=1839&amp;rft.aulast=Hobbes&amp;rft.aufirst=Thomas&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Farchive.org%2Fdetails%2Fenglishworkstho21hobbgoog&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AReason"></span>: "We must not therefore think that computation, that is, ratiocination, has place only in numbers, as if man were distinguished from other living creatures (which is said to have been the opinion of <i><a href="/wiki/Pythagoras" title="Pythagoras">Pythagoras</a></i>) by nothing but the faculty of numbering; for <i>magnitude, body, motion, time, degrees of quality, action, conception, proportion, speech and names</i> (in which all the kinds of philosophy consist) are capable of addition and substraction  [<i><a href="/wiki/Sic" title="Sic">sic</a></i>]. Now such things as we add or substract, that is, which we put into an account, we are said to <i>consider</i>, in Greek λογίζεσθαι [<i>logizesthai</i>], in which language also συλλογίζεσθι [<i>syllogizesthai</i>] signifies to <i>compute</i>, <i>reason</i>, or <i>reckon</i>."</span>
</li>

<li id="cite_note-46"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-46">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation book cs1 cs1-prop-long-vol" id="CITEREFRicco2015">Ricco, Robert B. (2015). "The development of reasoning".  In <a href="/wiki/Richard_M._Lerner" title="Richard M. Lerner">Lerner, Richard M.</a> (ed.). <i>Handbook of Child Psychology and Developmental Science</i>. Vol. 2. Cognitive Processes (7th ed.). Hoboken, NJ: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/John_Wiley_%26_Sons" title="John Wiley &amp; Sons">John Wiley &amp; Sons</a>. pp. 519–570 (534). <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Doi_(identifier)" title="Doi (identifier)">doi</a>:<a class="external text" href="https://doi.org/10.1002%2F9781118963418.childpsy213" rel="nofollow">10.1002/9781118963418.childpsy213</a>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/9781118136850" title="Special:BookSources/9781118136850"><bdi>9781118136850</bdi></a>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/OCLC_(identifier)" title="OCLC (identifier)">OCLC</a> <a class="external text" href="//www.worldcat.org/oclc/888024689" rel="nofollow">888024689</a>. <q>Moshman's (1990, 1998, 2004, 2013a) theory of the development of deductive reasoning considers changes in metacognition to be the essential story behind the development of deductive (and inductive) reasoning. In his view, reasoning involves explicit conceptual knowledge regarding inference (metalogical knowledge) and metacognitive awareness of, and control over, inference.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=bookitem&amp;rft.atitle=The+development+of+reasoning&amp;rft.btitle=Handbook+of+Child+Psychology+and+Developmental+Science&amp;rft.place=Hoboken%2C+NJ&amp;rft.pages=519-570+%28534%29&amp;rft.edition=7th&amp;rft.pub=John+Wiley+%26+Sons&amp;rft.date=2015&amp;rft_id=info%3Aoclcnum%2F888024689&amp;rft_id=info%3Adoi%2F10.1002%2F9781118963418.childpsy213&amp;rft.isbn=9781118136850&amp;rft.aulast=Ricco&amp;rft.aufirst=Robert+B.&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AReason"></span></span>

<li id="cite_note-63"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-63">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Davis is here using "poetic" in an unusual sense, questioning the contrast in Aristotle between action (<i>praxis</i>, the <i>praktikē</i>) and making (<i>poēsis</i>, the <i>poētikē</i>): "Human [peculiarly human] action is imitation of action because thinking is always rethinking. <li id="cite_note-72"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-72">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Example: Aristotle <i><a href="/wiki/Metaphysics_(Aristotle)" title="Metaphysics (Aristotle)">Metaphysics</a></i> <a class="external text" href="https://www.perseus.tufts.edu/hopper/text?doc=Perseus%3Atext%3A1999.01.0051%3Abook%3D1%3Asection%3D981b" rel="nofollow">981b</a>: τὴν ὀνομαζομένην σοφίαν περὶ τὰ πρῶτα αἴτια καὶ τὰς ἀρχὰς ὑπολαμβάνουσι πάντες: ὥστε, καθάπερ εἴρηται πρότερον, ὁ μὲν ἔμπειρος τῶν ὁποιανοῦν ἐχόντων αἴσθησιν εἶναι δοκεῖ σοφώτερος, ὁ δὲ τεχνίτης τῶν ἐμπείρων, χειροτέχνου δὲ ἀρχιτέκτων, αἱ δὲ θεωρητικαὶ τῶν ποιητικῶν μᾶλλον. <a class="external text" href="https://www.perseus.tufts.edu/hopper/text?doc=Perseus%3Atext%3A1999.01.0052%3Abook%3D1%3Asection%3D981b" rel="nofollow">English</a>: "...what is called Wisdom is concerned with the primary causes and principles, so that, as has been already stated, the man of experience is held to be wiser than the mere possessors of any power of sensation, the artist than the man of experience, the master craftsman than the artisan; and the speculative sciences to be more learned than the productive."</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-73"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-73">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><i>Metaphysics</i> <a class="external text" href="https://www.perseus.tufts.edu/hopper/text?doc=Perseus%3Atext%3A1999.01.0051%3Abook%3D1%3Asection%3D1009b" rel="nofollow">1009b</a> ποῖα οὖν τούτων ἀληθῆ ἢ ψευδῆ, ἄδηλον: οὐθὲν γὰρ μᾶλλον τάδε ἢ τάδε ἀληθῆ, ἀλλ᾽ ὁμοίως. διὸ Δημόκριτός γέ φησιν ἤτοι οὐθὲν εἶναι ἀληθὲς ἢ ἡμῖν γ᾽ ἄδηλον. <a class="external text" href="https://www.perseus.tufts.edu/hopper/text?doc=Perseus%3Atext%3A1999.01.0052%3Abook%3D1%3Asection%3D1009b" rel="nofollow">English</a> "Thus it is uncertain which of these impressions are true or false; for one kind is no more true than another, but equally so. And hence Democritus says that either there is no truth or we cannot discover it."</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-74"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-74">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">For example Aristotle <i>Metaphysics</i> <a class="external text" href="https://www.perseus.tufts.edu/hopper/text?doc=Perseus%3Atext%3A1999.01.0051%3Abook%3D1%3Asection%3D983a" rel="nofollow">983a</a>: ἐπεὶ δὲ φανερὸν ὅτι τῶν ἐξ <i>ἀρχῆς</i> αἰτίων δεῖ λαβεῖν <i>ἐπιστήμην</i> （τότε γὰρ εἰδέναι φαμὲν ἕκαστον, ὅταν τὴν <i>πρώτην</i> αἰτίαν οἰώμεθα <i>γνωρίζειν</i>） <a class="external text" href="https://www.perseus.tufts.edu/hopper/text?doc=Perseus%3Atext%3A1999.01.0052%3Abook%3D1%3Asection%3D983a" rel="nofollow">English</a> "It is clear that we must obtain knowledge of the <i>primary</i> causes, because it is when we think that we understand its <i>primary</i> cause that we claim to <i>know</i> each particular thing."</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-75"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-75">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Example: <i><a href="/wiki/Nicomachean_Ethics" title="Nicomachean Ethics">Nicomachean Ethics</a></i> <a class="external text" href="https://www.perseus.tufts.edu/hopper/text?doc=Perseus%3Atext%3A1999.01.0053%3Abekker+page%3D1139a" rel="nofollow">1139b</a>: ἀμφοτέρων δὴ τῶν <i>νοητικῶν</i> μορίων ἀλήθεια τὸ ἔργον. καθ᾽ ἃς οὖν μάλιστα ἕξεις ἀληθεύσει ἑκάτερον, αὗται ἀρεταὶ ἀμφοῖν. <a class="external text" href="https://www.perseus.tufts.edu/hopper/text?doc=Perseus%3Atext%3A1999.01.0054%3Abekker%20page%3D1139a" rel="nofollow">English</a> The attainment of truth is then the function of both the <i>intellectual</i> parts of the soul. Therefore their respective virtues are those dispositions that will best qualify them to attain truth.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-76"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-76">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Example: Plato <i><a href="/wiki/Republic_(Plato)" title="Republic (Plato)">Republic</a></i> <a class="external text" href="https://www.perseus.tufts.edu/hopper/text?doc=Perseus%3Atext%3A1999.01.0167%3Abook%3D6%3Asection%3D490b" rel="nofollow">490b</a>: μιγεὶς τῷ ὄντι ὄντως, γεννήσας νοῦν καὶ ἀλήθειαν, γνοίη <a class="external text" href="https://www.perseus.tufts.edu/hopper/text?doc=Perseus%3Atext%3A1999.04.0094%3Abook%3D6%3Asection%3D490b" rel="nofollow">English</a>: "Consorting with reality really, he would beget intelligence and truth, attain to knowledge"</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-77"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-77">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">"This quest for the beginnings proceeds through sense perception, reasoning, and what they call <i>noesis</i>, which is literally translated by "understanding" or intellect," and which we can perhaps translate a little bit more cautiously by "awareness," an awareness of the mind's eye as distinguished from sensible awareness." "Progress or Return" in An Introduction to Political Philosophy: Ten Essays by Leo Strauss. (Expanded version of Political Philosophy: Six Essays by Leo Strauss, 1975.) Ed. Hilail Gilden. Detroit: Wayne State UP, 1989.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-78"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-78">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">However, the empiricism of Aristotle must certainly be doubted. For example in <i>Metaphysics</i> 1009b, cited above, he criticizes people who think knowledge might not be possible because, "They say that the impression given through sense-perception is necessarily true; for it is on these grounds that both <a href="/wiki/Empedocles" title="Empedocles">Empedocles</a> and Democritus and practically all the rest have become obsessed by such opinions as these."</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-79"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-79">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">G.W.F. Hegel <a href="/wiki/Lectures_on_the_Philosophy_of_History" title="Lectures on the Philosophy of History"><i>The Philosophy of History</i></a>, p. 9, Dover Publications Inc., <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/0-486-20112-0" title="Special:BookSources/0-486-20112-0">0-486-20112-0</a>; 1st ed. 1899</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-:0-80"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-:0_80-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-:0_80-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation book cs1" id="CITEREFSharples2005">Sharples, R. W. (2005). <i>The Oxford companion to philosophy</i>. Ted Honderich (2nd ed.). Oxford: Oxford University Press. p. 896. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-0-19-153265-8" title="Special:BookSources/978-0-19-153265-8"><bdi>978-0-19-153265-8</bdi></a>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/OCLC_(identifier)" title="OCLC (identifier)">OCLC</a> <a class="external text" href="//www.worldcat.org/oclc/62563098" rel="nofollow">62563098</a>. <q>Moral virtue is the only good an wickedness the only evil...Emotions are interpreted in intellectual terms; those such as distress, pity (which is a species of distress), and fear which reflect false judgements about what is evil, are to be avoided (as also are those which reflect false judgement about what is good, such as love of honours or riches)...They did however allow the wise man such 'good feelings' as 'watchfulness' or kindness the difference being that these are based on sound (Stoic) reasoning concerning what matters and what does not.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=The+Oxford+companion+to+philosophy&amp;rft.place=Oxford&amp;rft.pages=896&amp;rft.edition=2nd&amp;rft.pub=Oxford+University+Press&amp;rft.date=2005&amp;rft_id=info%3Aoclcnum%2F62563098&amp;rft.isbn=978-0-19-153265-8&amp;rft.aulast=Sharples&amp;rft.aufirst=R.+W.&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AReason"></span></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-:1-81"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-:1_81-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-:1_81-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation book cs1" id="CITEREFRufus2000">Rufus, Musonius (2000). <i>Concise Routledge encyclopedia of philosophy</i>. Routledge. Virtue, a set of sciences governing moral choice, is the one thing of intrinsic worth and therefore genuinely 'good'.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=Concise+Routledge+encyclopedia+of+philosophy&amp;rft.place=London&amp;rft.pages=863&amp;rft.pub=Routledge&amp;rft.date=2000&amp;rft_id=info%3Aoclcnum%2F49569365&amp;rft.isbn=0-203-16994-8&amp;rft.aulast=Rufus&amp;rft.aufirst=Musonius&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AReason"></span></span>

</li>
<li id="cite_note-92"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-92">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation book cs1"><i>Natural Signs and Knowledge of God: A New Look at Theistic Arguments</i> (Reprint ed.). Oxford: Oxford University Press. 2012. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-0-19-966107-7" title="Special:BookSources/978-0-19-966107-7"><bdi>978-0-19-966107-7</bdi></a>.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=Natural+Signs+and+Knowledge+of+God%3A+A+New+Look+at+Theistic+Arguments&amp;rft.place=Oxford&amp;rft.edition=Reprint&amp;rft.pub=Oxford+University+Press&amp;rft.date=2012&amp;rft.isbn=978-0-19-966107-7&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AReason"></span></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-93"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-93">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation web cs1" id="CITEREFStephen_Jay_Gould1997"><a href="/wiki/Stephen_Jay_Gould" title="Stephen Jay Gould">Stephen Jay Gould</a> (1997). <a class="external text" href="http://www.stephenjaygould.org/library/gould_noma.html" rel="nofollow">"Nonoverlapping Magisteria"</a>. <i>www.stephenjaygould.org</i><span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">2016-04-06</span></span>. <q>To say it for all my colleagues and for the umpteenth millionth time (from college bull sessions to learned treatises): science simply cannot (by its legitimate methods) adjudicate the issue of God's possible superintendence of nature. We neither affirm nor deny it; we simply can't comment on it as scientists.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft.jtitle=www.stephenjaygould.org&amp;rft.atitle=Nonoverlapping+Magisteria&amp;rft.date=1997&amp;rft.au=Stephen+Jay+Gould&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.stephenjaygould.org%2Flibrary%2Fgould_noma.html&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AReason"></span></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-94"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-94">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation book cs1" id="CITEREFDawkins2008">Dawkins, Richard (2008). <a class="external text" href="https://archive.org/details/goddelusion00dawk_0" rel="nofollow">"4"</a>. <span class="cs1-lock-registration" title="Free registration required"><a class="external text" href="https://archive.org/details/goddelusion00dawk_0" rel="nofollow"><i>The God Delusion</i></a></span> (Reprint ed.). Mariner Books. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-0-618-91824-9" title="Special:BookSources/978-0-618-91824-9"><bdi>978-0-618-91824-9</bdi></a>. <q>This sounds terrific, right up until you give it a moment's thought. You then realize that the presence of a creative deity in the universe is clearly a scientific hypothesis. Indeed, it is hard to imagine a more momentous hypothesis in all of science. A universe with a god would be a completely different kind of universe from one without, and it would be a scientific difference. God could clinch the matter in his favour at any moment by staging a spectacular demonstration of his powers, one that would satisfy the exacting standards of science. Even the infamous Templeton Foundation recognized that God is a scientific hypothesis — by funding double-blind trials to test whether remote prayer would speed the recovery of heart patients. It didn't, of course, although a control group who knew they had been prayed for tended to get worse (how about a class action suit against the Templeton Foundation?) Despite such well-financed efforts, no evidence for God's existence has yet appeared.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=bookitem&amp;rft.atitle=4&amp;rft.btitle=The+God+Delusion&amp;rft.edition=Reprint&amp;rft.pub=Mariner+Books&amp;rft.date=2008&amp;rft.isbn=978-0-618-91824-9&amp;rft.aulast=Dawkins&amp;rft.aufirst=Richard&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Farchive.org%2Fdetails%2Fgoddelusion00dawk_0&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AReason"></span></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-95"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-95">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation journal cs1" id="CITEREFSeachris2009">Seachris, Joshua W. (April 2009). <a class="external text" href="https://www.pdcnet.org/pdc/bvdb.nsf/purchase?openform&amp;fp=philo&amp;id=philo_2009_0012_0001_0005_0023" rel="nofollow">"The Meaning of Life as Narrative: A New Proposal for Interpreting Philosophy's 'Primary' Question – Joshua W.
